Discussion Topics for Meetings of General Faculties Council (GFC) – Report of the Ad Hoc Working \nGroup (of the GFC Executive Committee)  \nMaterials before members are contained in the official meeting file. \nPresenter(s): Colin More, Member, Ad Hoc Working Group on GFC Discussion (and Member, GFC \nExecutive Committee); Steve Patten, Member, Ad Hoc Working Group on GFC Discussion (and Member, \nGFC Executive Committee); Fahim Rahman, Member, Ad Hoc Working Group on GFC Discussion (and \nMember, GFC Executive Committee) \nPurpose of the Proposal: To discuss a report provided by members of the Ad Hoc Working Group (of the \nGFC Executive Committee) tasked with considering, in detail, a proposal submitted by President Indira \nSamarasekera (and Chair, General Faculties Council (GFC)) prior to the September 8, 2014 meeting of the \nGFC Executive Committee regarding revitalization of discussion at GFC meetings. The documentation \nbefore members (ie, specifically, Attachment 1) summarizes members’ discussion on this issue at a \nmeeting called on September 29, 2014 and provides a series of reflections on how such discussions, as \nenvisioned by the President, might occur at future Council meetings. The Working Group presents this item \nto the GFC Executive Committee for further discussion and, in addition, members will be asked to \ndetermine ‘next steps’ for this item, including possible discussion at the November 24, 2014 GFC meeting. \nDiscussion: \nMr More provided members with an outline of the Report, noting that the members of the Working Group \nachieved consensus on the proposal, except for the issue of the introduction of Motions during the \nproposed discussion period. \nProfessor Patten explained that the difficulty in making a final determination with regard to rules around \nMotions introduced during this proposed discussion period is that the GFC Executive Committee retains \ndelegated authority over the agendas for GFC and, therefore, should retain some level of control over the \nissues brought forward during this period as well as selecting individuals to speak to the issues.  He \nrecommended a soft approach to the issue of Motions, including clarifying that, as set out in her original \nproposal, Dr Samarasekera did not envision Motions allowed during the course of the discussion period \nand that attempts to introduce Motions could be subsequently managed by the Chair.  He noted, as well, \nthat it is difficult to know in advance how many of these discussion periods can be added to GFC agendas \nin a given academic year. \nMr Rahman added that the Working Group did not establish mechanisms for vetting proposals, Motions, \nand how to schedule items into the discussion period. \nMembers provided a number of comments and questions in relation to this item, including, but not limited \nto:  that members may want to introduce Motions as a way to act on issues and that a mechanism to pre-\nempt this could be built into the rules surrounding the discussion period; a suggestion to present GFC \nmembers with these outstanding questions; clarification regarding the procedural rules of GFC in relation to \nthe introduction of Motions; that banning Motions outright will create other problematic issues and a \nsuggestion to clearly describe the mandate of this discussion period by explaining that it is about \ndiscussion and debate, not decision-making; and clarification on the best method to present the Report to \nmembers of GFC. \nThe Chair requested that a member of the Working Group summarize the exact proposal, so that it can be \npresented to GFC.     \nMr Bodnar provided members with advice regarding the presentation of the proposal at GFC. \nGFC Executive Committee 11/12/2014 \nPage 8 \n11. Goals (2014-2015) of the Students’ Union (SU) and the Graduate Students’ Association (GSA) – \nBrief Introduction  \n11.1 Goals from the Students’ Union (SU)  \nMaterials before members are contained in the official meeting file. \nPresenter: William Lau, President, Students’ Union (SU) \nPurpose of the Proposal: To communicate to the Board Learning and Discovery Committee (BLDC), the \nGFC Executive Committee, and General Faculties Council (GFC) the SU’s goals for the year (2014-2015) \nand how the SU Executive team plans to improve the life of students at the University of \nAlberta. \nDiscussion: \nMr Lau introduced himself to members and explained that the material provided clearly explains the \nStudents’ Union (SU) five key goals for 2014-15.  He provided members with more details in relation to \nthese goals, under the categories of:  Student Success; Affordability; Engaging Community; Growing \nPartnerships; and Building a Better Students’ Union.  \nHe explained that another focus will be on open educational resources, and Ms Orydzuk added that they \nhave instituted a campaign directed towards students and instructors in relation to electronic teaching \nmaterials, access codes to additional course resources, and working with Libraries to ensure that electronic \ntextbooks are available in the Libraries. She explained that the cost for access codes is onerous to \nstudents. \nMr Lau stated that they have focused on assessing their communication strategy towards the student body.  \nHe explained that the SU will attempt to broaden relationships with key partners with an aim to write a \nwhite paper about the undergraduate student experience.   \nDuring the discussion surrounding this item, members expressed a number of comments and questions, \nincluding, but not limited to:  clarification regarding the issues surrounding access codes; that there has \nbeen negative feedback from students with respect to e-resource materials and that affordability does not \nalways translate into better quality of education, according to Universal Student Ratings of Instruction \n(USRI) responses; clarification whether this discussion relates to open source textbooks or a publisher’s \nonline version of a textbook, as there is a difference between the two versions; that the platform for usage \nis usually problematic, as opposed to content; clarification surrounding how the SU will evaluate success \nfor these goals; and clarification on how the SU intends to increase more women within their organization. \n11.2 Goals from the Graduate Students' Association (GSA)  \nMaterials before members are contained in the official meeting file. \nPresenter: Nathan Andrews, President, Graduate Students’ Association (GSA) \nPurpose of the Proposal: To brief the Board Learning and Discovery Committee (BLDC), the GFC \nExecutive Committee, and General Faculties Council (GFC) on the key priorities for 2014-2015 identified \nby the GSA in its Strategic Work Plan (SWP). \nDiscussion: \nGFC Executive Committee 11/12/2014 \nPage 9 \nMr Andrews thanked members for their attention and provided them with a summary of the Graduate \nStudents’ Association (GSA) goals for 2014-15, including:  creating professional development opportunities \nfor graduate students; continued engagement surrounding financial support of post-secondary education; \nsupport for a strong Faculty of Graduate Studies and Research; continued partnerships in relation to the \nitems on the Final Report of the Graduate Student Supervision Task Force; increased services for \ninternational students; maintaining a strong advocacy presence in the areas of Municipal, Provincial and \nFederal Governments; and continued work with the University surrounding education, compliance, and \ndispute resolution associated with the Collective Agreement. He also noted that the GSA strives to \nmaintain their Bylaws and has created a successful program for graduate students to achieve training \nsurrounding governance and leadership. \n12. Proposal from the Office of the Vice-President (Research) and the Faculty of Graduate Studies and \nResearch to Create the UAPPOL Delay of Research Publication Procedure (with the Attendant \nDeletion of GFC Policy Manual Sections 96.1.1, 96.1.2, 96.1.3, 96.1.4, and 120.2) \nMaterials before members are contained in the official meeting file. \nPresenters: Glen Baker, Associate Vice-President (Research); Joanna Harrington, Associate Dean, Faculty \nof Graduate Studies and Research (FGSR) \nPurpose of the Proposal: To create a new UAPPOL procedure and to delete several attendant \nsub-sections of the GFC Policy Manual (ie, replaces GFC Policy Manual Sections 96.1.1, 96.1.2, 96.1.3, \n96.1.4, and 120.2); all references to the maximum period of delay for various types of research publications \nwill be contained in one UAPPOL procedure.  The reference to the delay/restricting access to the \npublication of theses will remain in the University Calendar, where the procedure for requesting a delay \nbeyond 12 months is outlined. \nDiscussion: \nDr Baker provided members with a brief summary of the proposal, adding that it is the result of \nconsiderable work and consultation carried out by the Office of the Vice-President (Research) and the \nFaculty of Graduate Studies and Research (FGSR). He noted that these changes reflect the complexities \nof clinical trials, which often slow down the analysis process.  He reported that there was a small working \ngroup established to discuss research publication delays and that the corresponding changes within the \nproposal represent their final recommendations. \nDr Harrington noted that the practices contained within the proposal are already standard for members of \nFGSR and that this is reflected in the appropriate regulations set out in the University Calendar.  She \nadded that it is also reflects in many ways what is standard practice at other western Canadian universities.  \nMembers, during the discussion surrounding this proposal, provided a number of comments and questions, \nincluding, but not limited to: clarification surrounding restrictions and sources of requests in relation to \ntheses and patenting; expressions of support for this proposal; and clarification as to whether the requests \nfor extensions were fairly straightforward. \nMotion: White/Rodgers \nTHAT the GFC Executive Committee approve, under delegated authority from the General \nFaculties Council, the new UAPPOL Delay of Research Publication Procedure, as submitted by the \nOffice of the Vice-President (Research) and the Faculty of Graduate Studies and Research and as set \nforth in Attachment 1, as well as the attendant deletion of GFC Policy Manual Sections 96.1.1, 96.1.2, \n96.1.3, 96.1.4, and 120.2 (as noted in Attachment 3). \nCARRIED \n5. Deletion of Section 65.3 (University of Alberta Student Housing Policy) from the GFC Policy Manual  \nMaterials before members are contained in the official meeting file. \nPresenter: Doug Dawson, Associate Vice-President (Ancillary Services), Facilities and Operations \nGFC Executive Committee 01/12/2015 \nPage 4 \nPurpose of the Proposal: To delete Section 65.3 of the GFC Policy Manual. The items referenced in this \nsection are outdated or historical in nature and current information is available in web and print sources.  In \naddition, this proposal facilitates the process of migrating material from the GFC Policy Manual and \nreducing redundancy across institutional policy documents.  Outdated policy will be rescinded/deleted, \nthereby aligning the University’s practices with current UAPPOL policy and operating practices, which have \nsuperseded the policies set out in the GFC Policy Manual. \nDiscussion: \nMr Dawson summarized the proposal by explaining that current provisions regarding student housing \nreside in University practice and policy and that, as a result of this, Section 65.3 of the GFC Policy Manual \nis now deemed redundant.   \nMs Orydzuk requested permission for a Visitor to speak on behalf of this item, and the Chair, who had \nbeen apprised of this request prior to the start of the meeting, consented. Mr Nicholas Diaz, Vice-President \n(Student Life), Students’ Union (SU), introduced himself to members and thanked the Chair for the \nopportunity to speak.  Mr Diaz noted that the Post-Secondary Learning Act grants legislative authority to \nGFC to make residence policy, with approval from the Board of Governors.  He cautioned members that \napproving this proposal as presented eliminates the opportunity of investigating an appropriate \nreplacement institutional policy surrounding student housing. \nMembers, during the discussion of this proposal, provided a number of comments and questions, including, \nbut not limited to: that GFC has engaged in broad review and discussion of this matter recently; that \ndeleting this policy and relying, instead, on numerous other digital sources for policy and process, such as \nwebsites, is not the same as having one comprehensive policy and that these external sources of \ninformation lack legislative authority; that enforcement is an issue and that the replacement sources do not \naccurately reflect the policy language; that it does not seem to make sense to delete one source of policy \nand spread it out over 13 different sources and that it is easier for students and administrators to have one \nplace to reference this policy; that there is a difference between policy statements, themselves, and how \nsuch policies are administered; that the Residence Community Standards relate more to judicial matters, \nso that there is a discontinuity between what is being deleted and what currently exists; a suggestion to \nrevise the existing policy; that the (proposed) deletion of sections of the GFC Policy Manual usually \naccompany proposals to transition the “old” policy into the UAPPOL environment; that it might be beneficial \nto review how GFC has delegated its powers over residences before proceeding; that this proposal \nappears not to be routine or urgent; and commentary that stakeholders have indicated that they are \nuncomfortable with this proposal and that there should be, instead, a replacement (updated) policy created \nand presented to full GFC. \nMr Bodnar reminded members that the GFC Executive Committee does have delegated authority from \nGFC over this matter, as set out in the proposal (and accompanying legislation) before the Committee. \nThe Chair, noting the nature of the discussion, suggested it might be wise at this time to table this item.  Dr \nElliott, in setting forth a 'Motion to Table', was afforded an opportunity, as set out within GFC's procedural \nrules, to make clear what his expectations were to move this proposal off the table and back to the \nCommittee for discussion.  He noted that he expected the existing policy would be replaced with a new, \ncurrent policy, created after consultation with key stakeholders.  The proposer, according to GFC's \nprocedural rules, was also allowed the opportunity to say what effect tabling the proposal would have; Mr \nDawson, when asked, declined to provide any further commentary. \nMotion: Elliott/More \nGFC Executive Committee 01/12/2015 \nPage 5 \nTHAT the GFC Executive Committee TABLE the proposed deletion of GFC Policy Manual Section 65.3 \n(University of Alberta Student Housing Policy), as originally submitted by Facilities and Operations. \nCARRIED \n6. Proposed Changes from the Office of the Registrar to Embedded Certificate Parchments  \nMaterials before members are contained in the official meeting file. \nPresenters: Lisa Collins, Vice-Provost and University Registrar; Corinne Callihoo, Assistant Registrar \n(Convocation and Ceremonies), Office of the Registrar \nPurpose of the Proposal: As a result of recent discussion with Provincial Government colleagues, it has \nbeen brought to the University’s attention that embedded credit certificates are not significantly \ndifferentiated from our freestanding credit certificates. Since embedded certificates do not require \nGovernment approval, it is recommended that the presentation of the embedded certificates be modified to \nvisually differentiate them from freestanding certificates that do receive governmental approval.  In addition, \nthe new design visually differentiates an embedded certificate from the parchment awarded students for \ncompletion of degree or diploma programs. As per UAPPOL policy, any substantive changes to \nparchments must be approved through the GFC Executive Committee.  The redesign will remove the coat \nof arms from the embedded certificate parchment and change the signatures from four (President, \nChancellor, Registrar, and Board Chair) to two (Registrar and Dean of the Faculty in which the certificate is \noffered). \nDiscussion: \nMs Collins reported to members that the Provincial Government has requested that the University of \nAlberta create a separate parchment for the completion of embedded certificate programs, as such \ncertificates are not recognized by the Government and should not be seen in the same light as the \nparchments awarded students for their completion of a diploma or degree program.  She stated that, in \nresponse to this request, Ms Callihoo created a working group to design such a document. \nMs Callihoo explained that offering a credential document to those completing certificate programs was \nformerly the responsibility of the individual Faculties and that, eventually, the Office of the Registrar was \nasked to participate in generating the document.  She noted that the new design for the embedded \ncertificates does not resemble a parchment and that it is their hope that the Provincial Government will \nagree to this new document. \nMembers, during the discussion in relation to this item, offered a number of comments and questions, \nincluding, but not limited to:  clarification regarding examples of an embedded certificate program; that the \nreasoning for the redesign seems unclear; and a query whether the Provincial Government has had an \nopportunity to review the latest iteration of the design. \nMotion:  Robertson/Molzahn \nTHAT the GFC Executive Committee approve, under delegated authority from General Faculties \nCouncil, the proposed changes to Embedded Certificate Parchments, as provided by the Office of the \nRegistrar and as set forth in Attachment 1, to be effective upon final approval. \nCARRIED \n7. Helping Individuals at Risk and Safe Disclosure and Human Rights Activity Reports 2013-2014  \nMaterials before members are contained in the official meeting file. \nPresenter: Mary Persson, Associate Vice-President (Audit and Analysis) \nPurpose of the Proposal: In 2008, the University introduced the Office of Safe Disclosure and Human \nRights (SDHR) as an intake office for issues arising under the Ethical Conduct and Safe Disclosure Policy. \nOrganizationally, the Office reports to the University Auditor to promote the independent, neutral, and \nadvisory nature of the function. \nIn 2010, the Helping Individuals at Risk Policy (HIAR) was introduced and an intake function was created to \nwork in conjunction with the Office of SDHR. The policy requires that annual reporting to General Faculties \nCouncil (GFC) and to the Board of Governors will occur after each year of operation. \nThe purpose of the two reports before members is to provide an overview of the activities of each office \nand to comment on trends and issues as well as to provide information on strategies for dealing with them. \nDiscussion: \nMs Persson provided members with a summary of the annual reports before them.  She explained that the \ncurrent data suggests that the offices act primarily in advisory roles, as opposed to complaint-handling \nroles, and that, further to this trend, these offices work closely with the Dean of Students to ensure that \ntheir programs are responsive and effective. She noted, as well, that the resources of the programs offered \nare being monitored to ensure that they are properly resourced. \nMembers expressed a number of questions and comments in relation to this report, including, but not \nlimited to: clarification surrounding the reasons why complaints have decreased; whether non-criminal \ndeaths on campus are included in this report; and clarification regarding information on international \nstudents set out within the report.  \n10. Annual Report on Undergraduate Enrolment 2014/15  \nMaterials before members are contained in the official meeting file. \nPresenters: Lisa Collins, Vice-Provost and University Registrar; Olive Yonge, Interim Provost and Vice-\nPresident (Academic) and Chair, GFC Executive Committee \nPurpose of the Proposal: To discuss the 2014/15 annual report on undergraduate enrolment. \nDiscussion: \nMs Collins explained that the Report has been adjusted according to feedback collected last year from \nmembers of the academy and that the primary objectives of this report are to track minimum admission \naverages and enrolment data in comparison to institutional and unit-specific targets and to provide \ninformation to the Faculties that assist them in future decision-making on enrolment management.  She \ncommented that, currently, some Faculties are under enrolled, while others are over enrolled.  \nShe stated that, although Chinese students represent a valued cohort of University of Alberta students, \nthere will be further focus on diversification in the future. \nShe explained that the Office of the Registrar is awaiting the decision from the Provincial Government on \nProvincial exam weighting and that, in addition, the conversation surrounding access continues. \nMs Collins provided members with a summary of the new offer acceptance process, which includes \noffering entrance awards at the time offers of admission are made to allow students to make better-\ninformed decisions. \nMembers, during the discussion surrounding the Report, provided a number of comments and questions, \nincluding, but not limited to:  clarification regarding the drop in completion rates; clarification regarding the \nnumbers of American students applying to the University of Alberta; clarification surrounding full-time \nstudent enrolment data; and clarification surrounding the data related to the applications from and \nenrolment of Chinese students. \n12. Student Mental Health and Wellness at the University of Alberta – Update  \nMaterials before members are contained in the official meeting file. \nPresenter: Robin Everall, Interim Vice-Provost and Dean of Students \nPurpose of the Proposal:  Given the ongoing importance of the issue of student mental health and wellness \nand the broad range of programs and initiatives that are ongoing at the University of Alberta, a presentation \nto General Faculties Council (GFC) to update the University community would be timely and useful.  \nKeeping the University community informed of a) the issue of mental health and wellness and b) what the \ninstitution is doing about it is crucially important.   \nA presentation to Council will: \nGFC Executive Committee 01/12/2015 \nPage 9 \n• Update all members of the community on the programs and initiatives underway; \n• Help raise awareness of what programs and services are available on campus; \n• Continue to lead the way in destigmatizing conversation around issues of mental health and wellness; \n• Continue to help establish support for a constructive and comprehensive approach to student mental \nhealth and wellness that will be proactive, effective, and sustainable over the long term. \nDiscussion: \nDr Everall explained that the issue of how to provide a supportive learning environment in terms of student \nmental health continues to be an important issue at the University of Alberta. She noted that, as academic \nsuccess and student mental health are tied together, there has been a philosophical shift in how the \ninstitution offers and delivers services and that there has been a shift, too, towards offering services in a \nmuch more timely manner to those in need. \nShe stated that the discussion also needs to factor in early intervention and academic support, as well as \nhelping individuals at risk; even though there are current structures in place addressing these issues,  \nstudents may not know about them. \nDuring the discussion in relation to this item, members expressed a number of comments and questions, \nincluding, but not limited to:  the status of continuing financial support from Alberta Health Services (AHS) \nfor mental health initiatives and services at the University of Alberta; whether the strategies currently in \nplace include better services for international students; and a suggestion to include in the GFC \npresentation more data about the prevalence of mental health issues in the institution’s current student \nbody. \nMs Swindlehurst, who was attending the meeting as an Observer, was invited by the Chair to explain to \nmembers that the Office of the President is interested in adding this important subject to the Agenda for \nGFC’s February 2, 2015 meeting, given the highlighted importance of student mental health for both the \nUniversity’s Board of Governors and the Senior Administration. \n13. Graduate Student Management Solution (GSMS) – Graduate Admissions Project  \nMaterials before members are contained in the official meeting file. \nPresenter: Mike MacGregor, Vice-Provost and Associate Vice-President (Information Technology)   \nPurpose of the Proposal: The Offices of the Vice-Provost and Associate Vice-President (Information \nTechnology) (VPIT), University Digital Strategies (UDS), and Faculty of Graduate Studies and Research \n(FGSR), in conjunction with the units offering graduate programs, initiated a project to review, change, and \nautomate all business processes concerning the graduate student application process at the University of \nAlberta. The primary objective is to acquire great students faster—to achieve that goal, the University is \nenabling a paperless solution.  The Graduate Admissions Project was deployed to improve the admissions \nprocess using a central, comprehensive, robust, and paperless admissions system that will enable users \n(student, administrative staff, and faculty) access to pertinent details. The Vice-Provost and Associate Vice-\nPresident (Information Technology) wishes to update members of the academy with regard to the progress \nmade to date on this important institutional initiative. \nDiscussion: \nDr MacGregor explained that this project represents one element of a larger program and that, while it is in \nthe early stages, there are two current issues at hand.  First, there is a need for the addition of qualified \nGFC Executive Committee 01/12/2015 \nPage 10 \ngraduate administrators; and, secondly, there are current delays in contract negotiations related to \nindemnification and code issues.  He explained that these issues are being addressed. \nA member enquired about the vision behind this project. \n14. Discussion Topics for Meetings of General Faculties Council (GFC) – Proposed (Final) Guidelines \nfrom the Ad Hoc Working Group (of the GFC Executive Committee)  \nMaterials before members are contained in the official meeting file. \nPresenters: Lisa Collins, Member, Ad Hoc Working Group on GFC Discussion (and Member, GFC \nExecutive Committee); Colin More, Member, Ad Hoc Working Group on GFC Discussion (and Member, \nGFC Executive Committee); Fahim Rahman, Member, Ad Hoc Working Group on GFC Discussion (and \nMember, GFC Executive Committee) \nPurpose of the Proposal:  To discuss and agree to proposed guidelines prepared by members of the GFC \nExecutive Committee’s Ad Hoc Working Group for discussion topics to go forward to General Faculties \nCouncil (GFC) in an organized and systematic manner.  The Working Group was tasked by the GFC \nExecutive Committee in September, 2014 with considering, in detail, a proposal submitted by President \nIndira Samarasekera (and Chair, GFC) prior to the September 8, 2014 meeting of the GFC Executive \nCommittee regarding revitalization of discussion at GFC meetings.   \nDiscussion: \nMr More provided members with an update with regard to the recent discussions held at meetings of the \nAd Hoc Working Group in relation to the introduction of a discussion period at GFC meetings. \nMs Collins noted that there was some debate surrounding the introduction of Motions during the proposed \ndiscussion periods and that the group deliberated on this matter at length as well as on the issue that there \nwas a sense at GFC that members want to use any mechanism to hold the University Administration \naccountable.  She commented that the intended point of the proposed discussion period is to provide \ninsightful and timely discussion on topics of interest and relevance to Council.  She noted that the group \nhad not determined when to discuss the issue again at GFC. \nMembers, during the ensuing discussion, provided a number of comments and questions, including but not \nlimited to:  what the next steps might be; clarification surrounding the introduction of Motions during these \ndiscussion periods; clarification regarding the selection of topics for discussion; whether a vote or general \nconsensus on the proposed guidelines was needed; clarification regarding future steps and timing.   \nMembers agreed, at the conclusion of discussion, that they were satisfied with the revised guidelines, as \npresented, and these should be deployed as the introduction of GFC discussion periods is rolled out.  The \nWorking Group would reconvene one last time to ensure the guidelines were in their final form and \ndetermine an appropriate timeline based on today’s discussion. \n15. Annual Report of the Appeals and Compliance Officer (2012-2014)  \nMaterials before members are contained in the official meeting file. \nPresenter: Michael Peterson, Appeals and Compliance Officer, University Governance \nPurpose of the Proposal: To provide the annual report of statistics, as required by GFC policy. \nDiscussion: \nMr Peterson introduced himself to members and thanked members for their attention.  He reported that the \nfindings within the Report cover the past two academic years.  He stated that the appeal system operates \nunder the highest levels of administrative fairness, as this is the final level of appeal at the University, and \nis subject to judicial review.  He noted that the appeal system can accommodate simple appeals as well as \nmore complicated ones. \nHe reported that there has been an increase in academic appeals, and that international students make up \na significant portion of these appeals.  He also added that more complex appeals are occurring.  He \nexplained that a large portion of his time goes towards preparing panel members, students, student groups \nand Faculties for these more complex appeals. \nHe reported that there have been three judicial reviews of appeals completed at the University of Alberta \nand all three affirmed the institutional processes in place. \nMr Peterson provided members with data in regards to specific charges and sanctions as contained within \nthe Report. \nMembers, during the discussion surrounding this Report, provided a number of comments and questions, \nincluding, but not limited to:  clarification regarding the appeal rates in certain Faculties; clarification about \nthe incidence of stronger sanctions being imposed following an appeal; whether there are administrative \nmethods being considered at the Faculty level to address appeals. \n7. He stated that this item appears on each Deans’ Council \nAgenda so that ongoing discussion and feedback surrounding this issue continue across the academy. Attachment 1 (pages 1 – 4)  A Vision for Innovation in Alberta – Progress Report, Faculty of Graduate \nStudies  \n“A Vision for Innovation in Alberta” \nGovernment of Alberta Grant  \nProgress Report  - January 2016 \nContext of Professional Development \nThe rapid growth in the number of graduate students in the past fifteen years has revealed a disjunction \nbetween academic training and the provincial, national, and global economy our students join on \ngraduation. Only a relatively small number of graduate students and postdoctoral fellows have the \nopportunity to become university professors. While percentages vary by discipline, most sources \nindicate that only about 15% of PhD students will enter the tenure track. The changing nature of \nacademic employment has spurred a global recognition that successful graduate programs must help \ngraduate students and postdocs transition into all sectors of work.  \nWe define Professional Development (PD) as the process by which graduate students and postdocs \nacquire skills that complement their disciplinary knowledge and technical skills. The University of \nAlberta has been ahead of other Canadian universities in this area for many years. We have offered PD \nworkshops and skills development for over a decade now. From 2013-2015 FGSR’s Professional \nDevelopment Advisory Board, a body with representatives from industry, non-profits, government, and \nthe academy, addressed skills and competencies, employer engagement, and university culture \nchange. That body’s work resulted in the Professional Development Strategic Plan, adopted by FGSR \nCouncil in Spring 2015 along with two mandatory program requirements: 1) an Individual Development \nPlan for all graduate students; 2) a dedicated 8-hour PD component that mirrors the ethics module. \nBoth are being piloted this year, with full implementation to take place in September 2016. These \nprogram requirements set the UofA apart from other Canadian and US universities. \nGraduate Students and Postdoctoral Fellows at UofA \nThe University of Alberta has approximately 600 postdoctoral fellows, 3000 PhD and 4600 Master’s \nstudents working in 72 departments across our campuses. Approximately one third of these students \nare international. Unlike some other universities in Canada, the Faculty of Graduate Studies at the UofA \ndoes not have responsibility for postdocs; however, all of our Professional Development opportunities \nare open to postdocs as well as students. \nGovernment of Alberta Grant \nOn March 10, 2015 the University received funding of $2,150,000 per year for three years to pilot PD \ninitiatives. The funding invests in four areas: \n1. Internships ($1,300,000) \n2. Professional Skills Development ($350,000) \n3. Entrepreneurship and Mentorship ($425,000) \n4. Curricular Change ($75,000). \nhttps://uofa.ualberta.ca/graduate-studies/about/resources-for-faculty-and-staff/uofa-professional-development-plan\n“A Vision for Innovation in Alberta” - Progress Report  І  page 2 \nThis report details our progress to date in all of these areas. It is important to note that we have \napproached each of these commitments in the spirit of partnership. We know that we will only be \nsuccessful if we collaborate with employers, alumni, other Alberta universities, student and postdoc \nassociations, and established entities like Mitacs and TEC Edmonton. The FGSR is grateful for the \ngenerous participation of all its PD partners. \nGovernance Model and Commitments \nThis diagram represents the grant commitments and our key partners. We envisage reconstituting the \nProfessional Development Advisory Board with representatives from industry, non-profits, government, \nalumni, students, postdocs and professors. This Board will meet approximately quarterly and offer \nadvice on the overall direction of the PD initiative. \nAt the centre of the diagram sits the key “doer”: the Project Manager, Andrea Graham. She sits on the \nFGSR Dean’s Executive Committee along with the FGSR Associate Deans, the Assistant Dean, the \nOutreach Manager, the SFO and the Dean. This group provides oversight of key academic matters and \nis more hands-on than the Advisory Board. \n“A Vision for Innovation in Alberta” - Progress Report  І  page 3 \nProgress on Specific Initiatives  \nInternships: We tackled internships first because it was clear that this was the area of greatest interest \nfrom students and potential employers. It is also the area of greatest investment from the Government \nof Alberta, and the area of greatest complexity. (Considerable time has been spent aligning the terms of \nthe GoA grant with UofA finance, HR policies, and the GSA Collective Agreement.) \nThe Graduate Student Internship Advisor, who works for FGSR on this grant but is located in the \nCareer Centre, has been finalizing existing partnerships and developing new ones. The goal is a \ndiverse range of work experience opportunities which will allow graduate students to develop hands-on \nexperience outside of academia and through which they can better understand how transferable their \nunique skills really are. By January 2016, three external placements will have begun with IPAC, \nCampus Alberta Neuroscience, and the Alberta Urban Municipalities Association, with the possibility for \nthree to five more to start early spring with their partnering agencies. Another four to five placements \nwill be posted by 1 Feb 2016 within various Government of Alberta departments and partnering \ncommunity agencies. Building on existing partnerships and internship structures will allow us to create \n20 placements per year with IMPORTTEN and up to 50 per year with MITACs. \nInternal placements have been posted for the Faculty of Engineering and the Office of the President, \nwith additional placements planned with the Office of Safe Disclosure and Human Rights, Employment \nEquity, Student Ombud Service, and Community Service Learning. \nProfessional Skills Development: This is an area in which the UofA shines. Because of the efforts of \nDr Renee Polziehn, we have been offering PD workshops to nearly 2000 participants per year for a \ndecade. In addition to these in-person offerings, we have both piloted and purchased online modules \nfor our students’ use: UofA is now the only non-Ontario university with access to MyGradSkills, an \nintegrated PD site developed by a consortium of Ontario universities, and we have streamlined the \nSkillSoft modules available through HRS, too. \nGiven our strength in this area already, our Skills Development emphasis for the government grant \nfocuses on developing collaborative opportunities for grad skills across the Campus Alberta Research \nIntensive universities (UofA, UofC, Lethbridge and Athabasca). We have developed a three-phase \nCampus Alberta Professional Development Initiative that will supplement institutional programs and \ndevelop new inter-university modules that reach across the CARI sector.  \n• Phase 1: Develop matrix, tools, gap inventory, template for e-portfolios, identify metrics, and \nengage graduate students, postdocs and industry representatives (Jan-July 2016) \n• Phase 2: Annual Career Development Conference for graduate students and postdocs (Aug \n2016) \n• Phase 3: Web Portal (2016-17). \nThis Professional Development Initiative will be competitively distinct from programs offered in other \nprovinces. It is designed to be flexible yet coordinated, offering cross-institutional access to students \nand postdocs across Campus Alberta. Its training modules will be offered through a combination of \nonline, in-person and blended delivery mechanisms that offer metrics related to program learning \nobjectives and outcomes. \nEntrepreneurship and Mentorship: The UofA is fortunate to exist in an environment where \nentrepreneurship has already taken shape through some key bodies, and where a rich environment of \nalumni and partners in industry, non-profits and government stand ready to engage with our students \n“A Vision for Innovation in Alberta” - Progress Report  І  page 4 \nand postdocs. Nowhere are we more reliant on our partners than in the area of entrepreneurship and \nmentorship. \nAlberta Venture Mentoring Services has developed a plan for expanding its entrepreneur-focused, \ngroup mentorship model to serve the majority of our graduate students and postdocs. VMS will engage \nthree constituencies in a tiered program: \n• Group 1 (several hundred): Graduate Students (GS)/Postdoctoral Fellows (PDFs) who are \ninterested in learning more about entrepreneurship and whether it is right for them; GS/PDFs \nwho want to hear from alumni who have pursued interesting career paths; GS/PDFs who might \nhave a business idea or technology at a very early stage of ideation. \n• Group 2 (several dozen): GS/PDFs who have a business idea or technology that is past ideation \nstage, who are seeking commercial possibilities or require external assessment of the visibility \nof their idea/technology; GS/PDFs who need specific advice on topics relation to personal \ndevelopment as an entrepreneur. \n• Group 3 (12-20 per year): GS/PDFs who are well into the process of commercializing their \nproduct/technology; GS/PDFs who self-identify as entrepreneurs; GS/PDFs who require \nsupport, feedback and coaching as it relates to running and growing a business.  \nVMS currently offers support to students at the Group 3 level only, so funding from the PD grant will \nallow us to build out offerings to students at the Group 1 and 2 levels.  \nWe continue to develop partnership possibilities with TEC Edmonton, eHUB and UofA’s alumni \norganizations. \nCurricular Change: In some respects, curricular change is the most important and the most complex \narea of endeavour, because disciplinary curricula are so closely enmeshed with academic culture. As \nwe note in the Professional Development Strategic Plan, the University community has to address \nsome tacit but false assumptions, such as:   \n• graduate students and postdocs will pick up all the non-disciplinary skills necessary for success \nin the job market through passive learning;  \n• any career other than that of a tenured university professor is non-traditional, and so unusual in \nsome way;   \n• non-disciplinary skills are only relevant to non-professorial careers;  \n• no graduate student or postdoc can find the time for professional development without either \njeopardizing their academic work or adding significantly to the completion time of their degree. \nTo change the curriculum is to change the culture, and this is a process that must unfold slowly, with \nample consultation. Curricular changes might affect coursework (particularly in the PhD), the form of \nand intended audience for the dissertation, supervisory committees (should non-academic mentors be \nadmitted, e.g.?), the status of full-time vs part-time study, disciplinarity, the status of teaching skills, and \ntime to completion. Graduate degrees have always varied widely, which means that change is likely to \ntake the form of a series of experiments, the outcomes of which may not be known in advance. Buy-in \nwill certainly only come from the ground up. We are working with Dr Sarah Stein Greenberg from the \nStanford d.School on a “design thinking” approach to doctoral curriculum, as a first step. \nItem No. 7 \nGENERAL FACULTIES COUNCIL \nFor the Meeting of January 25, 2016 \nOUTLINE OF ISSUE \nAgenda Title: Annual Report on Undergraduate Enrolment 2015/16 \nMotion:  N/A  \nItem   \nAction Requested Approval Recommendation  Discussion/Advice Information \nProposed by Lisa Collins, Vice-Provost and University Registrar \nPresenters Steven Dew, Provost and Vice-President (Academic); Lisa Collins, Vice-\nProvost and University Registrar \nSubject Discussion of the annual report on undergraduate enrolment  \nDetails \nResponsibility Provost and Vice-President (Academic) \nThe Purpose of the Proposal is \n(please be specific) \nTo discuss the 2015/16 annual report on undergraduate enrolment. \nThe Impact of the Proposal is Provides the institution with undergraduate enrolment information for \nawareness and to aid in future planning.  \nReplaces/Revises (e.g., \npolicies, resolutions) \nN/A \nTimeline/Implementation Date N/A  \nEstimated Cost N/A \nSources of Funding N/A \nNotes N/A \nAlignment/Compliance \nAlignment with Guiding \nDocuments \nDare to Discover Values: to provide an intellectually superior educational \nenvironment; integrity, fairness, and principles of ethical conduct built on \nthe foundation of academic freedom, open inquiry, and the pursuit of \ntruth. \nCompliance with Legislation, \nPolicy and/or Procedure \nRelevant to the Proposal \n(please quote legislation and \ninclude identifying section \nnumbers) \n1. Six-Year Program Completion Rates, Undergraduate Direct Entry \nAnnual Report on Undergraduate Enrolment 2015/16 1 \nMESSAGE FROM THE VICE-PROVOST AND UNIVERSITY REGISTRAR \nThe 2015/16 enrolment cycle marked significant change in undergraduate enrolment management at the \nUniversity of Alberta.  Success in undergraduate enrolment depends on the university’s ability to meet its \napproved enrolment targets in the face of significant student demand for most undergraduate programs.  \nSuccess also means attracting top students and providing access to a diverse student body (including \nunderserved populations) from across Alberta, Canada, and the world. Finally, success must include \nretaining those students through to completion of their programs and transition to alumni status.  \nThe environment in which the university recruits and admits its students has become increasingly \ncompetitive, given demographic changes that include a reduction in the number of 18-year-olds across the \ncountry, a trend that is expected to continue across Canada for another 10 years. In this environment, well-\nqualified Albertan students are sought after by other top Canadian universities recruiting actively within our \nprovince. Due to the strength of Alberta’s K-12 education system, our high school graduates are often \ngranted specific advantages in admissions processes at universities in British Columbia and Ontario as they \nwork to entice them.  \nAlbertan students also have a wide range of choices among institutions within their home province, as do \ntheir out-of-province counterparts in their own provinces. International markets are tighter than in previous \nyears, with many destination countries increasing active recruitment and some traditional source countries \n(like China) taking deliberate steps to make studying in one’s home country more appealing than ever. With \nso many options available to our prospective students, the University of Alberta must put its best foot \nforward to raise awareness, cultivate positive perceptions, and highlight the range and quality of academic \nprograms available at this university. \nThis year we are able to see the impact of the university’s “10 Point Enrolment Management Plan,” that \nincluded a deliberate shift away from highly conditional early admission offers and toward firm admission \noffers based on the earliest reliable grade information. High school applicants may now receive a firm offer \nof admission (in all programs except Engineering) based on strong Grade 11 marks. Applicants also have a \nchance to demonstrate competitive admissibility throughout their Grade 12 year, right up until their final \nGrade 12 marks are released. Because these offers are most often firm (as opposed to requiring strict \nmaintenance of a prescribed grade average), we saw a significant drop in offer revocation in most faculties.  \nIn the same way that the University of Alberta makes firm commitments to its admitted students, we now \nask for the same in return.  In 2015/16, the university began asking admitted students to formally accept \ntheir admission offers by a specified deadline.  These changes provide additional certainty for students as \nwell as provide better data to faculties managing incoming student enrolment numbers and central service \nunits preparing for the incoming class. \nAnnual Report on Undergraduate Enrolment 2015/16 2 \nThe overall enrolment picture for the 2015/16 cycle is one of steady state. While some faculties seek to \nrecruit additional qualified students, many are managing total enrolment downward in a controlled fashion to \ncorrect previous over subscription to their programs. The university expects to finish the 2015/16 academic \nyear enrolled very close to its total undergraduate enrolment targets. Where demand is high, entrance \naverages will inevitably climb, and this has been the experience at the University of Alberta over the past \ndecade. Still, there are so many learner pathways for Albertan students to choose from, including post-\nsecondary transfer routes to the University of Alberta, so we are aware of our need for increased \nrecruitment to continue attracting strong applicants. \nWhere appropriate, this report includes multi-year trend data. For the first time, we are now able to include \n10-year trends in some categories to support broader discussions that look back as well as forward. \nThe University of Alberta continues to attract a large proportion of our domestic students from within \nAlberta, but many of our students also come from British Columbia, Ontario and Saskatchewan. \nAdditionally, we enrol some students from Manitoba as well as the other provinces and territories. The past \ntwo years represented a small increase in out-of-province enrolment, relative to the years prior.  \nOur international undergraduate student ratio of 14 per cent (stable in 2014/15 and 2015/16) is approaching \nthe institutional undergraduate target of 15 per cent. This is the result of sustained effort across the \nuniversity to attract international students from around the world. “Maple Leaf” students, or study-permit \nstudents already in Canada, have a powerful impact on our international applicant pool – about a quarter of \nthe international students on campus today are in this group.  \nAboriginal students remain a priority enrolment group. This year the university experienced a small increase \nin undergraduate Aboriginal students. Numbers are based on self-identification by students at the time of \nadmission. While there remains much work to be done to achieve a representative Aboriginal population in \nour student numbers, we are pleased to see modest growth and continue to actively recruit Aboriginal \nstudents. In 2015/16, the admission rate for Aboriginal applicants was 63 per cent, which is higher than the \n59 per cent admission rate overall.   \nFirst year undergraduate retention rate remains strong at 87.6 per cent of our domestic first year students \nand 84.1 per cent of our international students returning for their second year of study. Overall first year \nretention rate among domestic and international students is 86.9 per cent, an increase of 0.7 per cent over \nthe previous year sustaining the long term upward trend over the past seven years.   \nThis report is, by nature, retrospective.  Its annual publication and related discussion are intended as \nassessment tools to gauge how well the University of Alberta is succeeding in monitoring and managing its \nundergraduate enrolment. Looking forward, the university can continue to plan and manage on a solid \nfoundation. I’ll look forward to reporting again next year to share news of the 2016/17 cycle, one that is \nalready very active at time of writing. \nYour feedback and questions are welcome; please contact me at ualberta.registrar@ualberta.ca \nSincerely, \nLisa M. Collins \nVice-Provost & University Registrar \nmailto:ualberta.registrar@ualberta.ca\nAnnual Report on Undergraduate Enrolment 2015/16 3 \n1. TOTAL UNDERGRADUATE ENROLMENT  \n1.1 ENROLMENT HEADCOUNT \nTotal undergraduate enrolment has been persistently above 30,000 students over the past several years. \nUndergraduate headcount in the 2015/16 academic year is 30,626, a two per cent decrease over \n2014/15 enrolment.  \nThere has been a purposeful correction of over enrolment from previous years in at least five faculties. The \nuniversity has moved from being overenrolled by an average of 2.5 per cent across all faculties in 2014/15 \nto undergraduate enrolment in 2015/16 being much closer to targets in most faculties (an average of 0.3 per \ncent over target across faculties).  \nFigure 1: Enrolment Headcount (2011 to 2015)   \nSource: Office of the Registrar, December 1 REGSTATS Archive \nNote: Undergraduate headcount includes Postgraduate Medical and Dental Education \n 7,474   7,598   7,664   7,572   7,204  \n 31,300   31,904   31,648   31,161   30,626  \n -\n 5,000\n 10,000\n 15,000\n 20,000\n 25,000\n 30,000\n 35,000\n2011/12 2012/13 2013/14 2014/15 2015/16\nGraduate Undergraduate\nAnnual Report on Undergraduate Enrolment 2015/16 4 \n1.2 NEW AND CONTINUING REGISTRATION, UNDERGRADUATE HEADCOUNT  \nTotal undergraduate registration is comprised of “new-to-program” intake (applicants registered) and \ncontinuing students (continuing registered). APPLICANT NUMBERS, QUALITY AND YIELD \n2.1 APPLICANT NUMBERS \nAfter recovering from the 2008/09 recession, applicant numbers continued to climb at an average of 1,050 \napplicants per year. However, in what looks like a reenactment of the 2008/2009 scenario, applicant \nnumbers dropped by two per cent (535 persons) in 2015/16.   \nFigure 4: Ten Year Undergraduate Applicant Curve  \nSource: Strategic Analysis and Data Warehousing, Applicant and Enrolment Management Report, Enrolment Management Table  \nNotes:  \n1. Over the past ten years, admission rate has remained in the range of 57 per cent \nto 63 per cent with an average of 60 per cent. Admission rate is influenced by enrolment targets, quality of \nthe applicant pool, and expected yield rate (see section 2.3).  \nFigure 5: Ten Year Undergraduate Admission Rate Curve \nSource: Strategic Analysis and Data Warehousing, Applicant and Enrolment Management Report, Enrolment Management Table  \nNotes:  \n1. There are multiple factors in yield rate; \nthe observed decline in yield rate over the past six years is due in part to increases in admission averages. \nAs the university has become more selective (targeting highly qualified applicants), this has meant a shift to \na more competitive applicant category. As highly qualified applicants often have multiple admission offers \nfrom other institutions, the number of admitted applicants who register at the university decreases.  \nFigure 6: Ten Year Undergraduate Yield Rate Curve \nSource: Strategic Analysis and Data Warehousing, Applicant and Enrolment Management Report, Enrolment Management Table  \nNotes:  \n1. The Faculty of Arts as \nwell as Agriculture, Life & Environmental Sciences had a two per cent decrease in the competitive averages \nof some programs. The Faculty of Education had a five per cent increase while Nursing applied between \ntwo and three per cent increase across its programs.  \nOverall, the simultaneous drop in applicant numbers and yield rate (see sections 2.1 and 2.3) meant that \nadmission averages remained relatively stable for many faculties in 2015/16 to ensure the university was \nable to meet its enrolment target. \nTable 3: Ten Year Competitive Admission Average History (2006 -2015) \nFaculty  2006 2007 2008 2009 2010 2011 2012 2013 2014 2015 \nAgric., Life & Environ Sciences 70 70 70 70 70-75 70-75 70-75 70-80 78-82 78-80 \nArts 70 70 70 70 70 70-75 70-75 72-75 72-80 70-80 \nAugustana Faculty 65 70 70 70 70 70 70 70 70 70 \nEducation \n70-90 75-80 70-75 70-80 \nEngineering 80 80 80 80 80 80 81 83 85-90 85-90 \nFaculty of Native Studies 70 70 70 70 70 70 70 70 70 70 \nNursing 70.2-78 70-75 75-76 74-75 75 74-75 75-78 76-80 78-80 80-83 \nPhysical Educ. & Recreation 70-74 70-78 70-76 70-78 70-78 74-81 75-80 75-82 75-81 74-82 \nFaculte Saint-Jean 70 70 70 70 70 70 70 70 70 70 \nScience 75-80 75-80 73-80 71-80 72-80 75-80 76-80 80-85 82-85 82-85 \nSource: Office of the Registrar.  \nNote:  The Faculty of Education started direct entry admissions in 2012 \nAnnual Report on Undergraduate Enrolment 2015/16 12 \n2.5 MEAN ADMISSION AVERAGES, UNDERGRADUATE DIRECT ENTRY \nThere is high demand for our direct entry faculties, a reflection of our accessibility and our strong reputation \nas a Top 5 research institution. Mean admission average refers to the mean score of admitted high school \napplicants in required high school courses. An increase in mean admission average would suggest an \nimprovement in the quality of the high school applicant pool. Among the 10 direct entry faculties, five \nfaculties witnessed a one percentage point decrease in mean admission averages in 2015/16 relative to the \nprevious year. However, there were 403 fewer direct entry registrations, indicating a \nlower yield rate among the admitted than in the preceding years.  \nDirect entry yield rates (registrants as per cent of admits) stood at 66 per cent in 2011/12 and 56 per cent in \n2014/15. Direct entry yield rate in 2015/16 is 48 per cent. As shown in section 2.9, highly qualified \napplicants often yield at a lower rate than the less qualified. Highly qualified applicants are more likely to \nreceive multiple offers of admission from other universities. Therefore, as the University of Alberta \nincreasingly targets highly qualified applicants, it is in competition with the world’s best universities to attract \nthese students.  \nFigure 7: Direct Entry Applicant, Admission and Registration Numbers (2011 – 2015)   \nSource: Strategic Analysis and Data Warehousing, Applicant and Enrolment Management Report. \n 11,721  \n 13,071  \n 14,566  \n 15,109   14,946  \n 7,636  \n 8,161   8,633   8,560  \n 9,116  \n 5,024   5,331   5,111   4,821   4,418  \n -\n 2,000\n 4,000\n 6,000\n 8,000\n 10,000\n 12,000\n 14,000\n 16,000\n2011/12 2012/13 2013/14 2014/15 2015/16\nApplicants Applicants Admitted Applicants Registered\nAnnual Report on Undergraduate Enrolment 2015/16 14 \n2.7 APPLICANT YIELD, UNDERGRADUATE POST SECONDARY TRANSFER \nPost secondary transfer applicant and admission trends are very similar to those for direct entry students. \nApplicant numbers declined by 239 persons in 2015/16 compared with the 2014/15 enrolment cycle, but \n116 more offers were made in 2015/16 than in 2014/15.  \nPost-secondary transfer yield numbers (that is the numbers registered) showed a moderate increase in \n2015/16 compared to 2014/15, but remained lower than in 2011/12 and 2012/13.    \nYield rates among post secondary applicants have been relatively higher and more stable than those for \ndirect entry applicants. Nearly three quarters of admitted post secondary transfer applicants consistently \nchoose to register at the University of Alberta.  Post secondary yield rates increased moderately from 73 per \ncent in 2014/15 to 74 per cent in 2015/16.   \nFigure 8: Post Secondary Transfer Applicant, Admission and Registration Numbers (2011 – 2015)   \nSource: Strategic Analysis and Data Warehousing, Applicant and Enrolment Management Report. \nNote:  Data in the above chart does NOT include Internal Transfer applicants.  \n 8,404   8,607   8,750  \n 8,846   8,607  \n 4,114   4,057   3,810   3,681   3,797  \n 3,137   3,097   2,792   2,701   2,808  \n -\n 1,000\n 2,000\n 3,000\n 4,000\n 5,000\n 6,000\n 7,000\n 8,000\n 9,000\n 10,000\n2011/12 2012/13 2013/14 2014/15 2015/16\nApplicants Applicants Admitted Applicants Registered\nAnnual Report on Undergraduate Enrolment 2015/16 15 \n2.8 ADMISSION TIMING AND YIELD RATE, UNDERGRADUATE DIRECT ENTRY \nYield rate, the proportion of admitted applicants who register, is affected by a number of factors, including \nthe need for timely admission offers, the extension of incentives to highly qualified applicants, the ability of \nadmitted international applicants to obtain immigration documentation, as well as the overall attractiveness \nof the university. The University of Alberta is in competition with other institutions. \nAmong 2015/16 domestic applicants, earlier offers yielded at a higher rate than later offers, but the \ndifference in yield rate was not statistically significant. Among international applicants, no statistical \ndifference was detected in the yield rate between earlier and later offers. That said, it is fully acknowledged \nthat earlier offers are a priority. Offer completion is dependent on a number of factors including submission \nof all required documentations, quality of applicants, as well as RO admission and faculty processing \ntimelines. \nFigure 9: Yield Rate by Month of Admission among High School Applicants (2015/16 Admission Cycle)   \nSource: Office of the Registrar.  \nAnnual Report on Undergraduate Enrolment 2015/16 16 \n2.9 ADMISSION AVERAGES AND YIELD RATE, UNDERGRADUATE DIRECT ENTRY  \nIn the 2015/16 enrolment cycle, the effect of admission averages appeared to be one of the strongest \nfactors on yield rate. Although this preliminary analysis is not yet conclusive due to having only one year of \ndata, it may be early indication of a trend.  \nFor instance, among domestic direct entry applicants admitted into their first choice faculties in December, \nadmission averages explained 93 per cent of the variation in yield rate between admission average bands \nas shown below. Among those admitted in April, admission averages explained 87 per cent of the variation. \nLikewise, among international applicants admitted into their first choice faculties in December, admission \naverages explained 74 per cent of the variation in yield rate between admission average bands. Among \nthose admitted in April, admission averages explained 46 per cent of the variation.   \nAlthough the specific effect of admission averages on yield rate was found to vary from faculty to faculty and \nfrom month to month, the general trend observed in 2015/16 suggested a strong inverse relationship \nbetween admission averages and yield.  \nFigure 10: Yield Rate by Admission Average Band among High School Applicants (2015/16 Admission Cycle)   \nSource: Office of the Registrar.  \nAnnual Report on Undergraduate Enrolment 2015/16 17 \n2.10   ADMISSION REVOCATION RATES, UNDERGRADUATE DIRECT ENTRY \nUp to and including 2014/15, applicants were offered early conditional admission but were also required to \nmaintain a competitive average in order to retain the offer. As a result, offer revocation rates were as high \nas 15 per cent in some faculties, and had been even higher in previous years. Students with revoked offers \nreported high levels of distress.  \nFor 2015/16, all faculties except Engineering made a policy change that allowed extension of firm offers of \nadmission by meeting a competitive average based on either Grade 11 marks, a combination of Grade 11 \nand Grade 12, or Final Grade 12 marks. As expected the revocation rate has fallen dramatically to three \npercent, resulting in increased predictability for students.  \nThe relatively high offer revocation rate for the Faculty of Engineering is reflective of its continued use of the \nprevious conditional admissions policy.   \nFigure 11: Admission Revocation Rates in 2015/16 Compared with the Previous Academic Year   \nSource: Office of the Registrar.  \nAnnual Report on Undergraduate Enrolment 2015/16 18 \n3. INTERNATIONAL UNDERGRADUATE ENROLMENT \nAs faculties manage downwards to enrolment targets, the admissions environment becomes more \ncompetitive for both domestic and international applicants. The current steady state of our international \nstudent population is a positive reflection of the university’s strong reputation and recruitment efforts both \nwithin and outside Canada. \n3.1 INTERNATIONAL ENROLMENT HEADCOUNT, UNDERGRADUATE \nThe university aims to reach an international undergraduate enrolment of 15 per cent. International \nenrolment increased steadily from 9.4 per cent in 2011/12 to 14 per cent in 2014/15. While no further gain in \ninternational enrolment proportion was made in 2015/16, the 14 per cent achieved in the previous year was \nsustained.  \nFigure 12: International Enrolment Headcounts and Proportions in Total Enrolment (2011 to 2015)   \nSource: Office of the Registrar, December 1 REGSTATS Archive \nNotes: \n1. RETENTION AND COMPLETION RATES \n5.1. STUDENT RETENTION, YEAR 1 TO YEAR 2, UNDERGRADUATE \nRetention rates among first year undergraduates indicate the proportion of first year registered students \nwho return to the university for their second year of study. Students returning for a second year of study \neither return to their first year faculty or transfer to a different faculty.  \nThe proportion of first year undergraduate students who transfer to a different faculty has varied between \n12.7 and 14.5 per cent over the past 8 years. The proportion of first year students returning to their first year \nfaculty increased gradually over the past four academic cycles from 70.3 per cent in 2012/13 to 73.9 per \ncent in 2015/16.  \nTotal retention rate is comprised of those who return to their first year faculty and those who transfer to a \ndifferent faculty. There has been a gradual increase in first year undergraduate retention rate over the \nyears, from 82.2 per cent in 2008/09 to 85 per cent in 2011/12 and 86.2 per cent in 2014/15. In the 2015/16 \nacademic year, 86.9 per cent of the first year cohort of 2014/15 undergraduates returned.   \nThe gradual increase in first year retention rate over the years means a gradual decrease in the proportion \nof first year cohorts who do not return. Of the 13.1 per cent that did not return in 2015/16, nearly half (42 per \ncent) had been required to withdraw. The remainder of the non-returnees dropped out for reasons other \nthan requirement to withdraw.  \nFigure 21: Proportions of First Year Undergraduates who Returned for their Second Year of Study (2007 to 2015)   \nSource: Strategic Analysis and Data Warehousing, Applicant and Enrolment Management Report, Retention Rates Table.  \n70.4% \n67.9% 70.1% 70.0% 70.5% 70.3% \n72.2% 73.2% 73.9% \n13.4% 14.3% 14.0% 14.4% 14.5% 14.4% 12.7% 13.0% 13.0% \n83.8% 82.2% 84.1% \n84.4% 85.0% 84.7% 84.9% \n86.2% 86.9% \n0.0%\n10.0%\n20.0%\n30.0%\n40.0%\n50.0%\n60.0%\n70.0%\n80.0%\n90.0%\n100.0%\n2007/08 2008/09 2009/10 2010/11 2011/12 2012/13 2013/14 2014/15 2015/16\nR\net\nu\nrn\ned\nReport Year \nReturned to Same Faculty Returned to a Different Faculty Total Retention Rate\nAnnual Report on Undergraduate Enrolment 2015/16 28 \n5.2. STUDENT RETENTION, YEAR 1 TO YEAR 2, DOMESTIC UNDERGRADUATE   \nTotal retention rate among first year domestic undergraduates is very similar to the overall first year \nundergraduate retention rate discussed in section 5.1. Between 83.1 and 86.4 per cent of first year \ndomestic undergraduates have historically returned for their second year of study and there has been a \ngradual increase in the retention rate over the years. In 2015/16, retention rate among the domestic cohort \nincreased further to reach 87.6 per cent.  \nHowever, compared to the overall group the rate of faculty change among domestic returnees is slightly \nlower meaning that a slightly higher proportion of domestic returnees return to their first year faculty. In \n2015/16, 75.3 per cent of the previous year’s cohort of first year domestic undergraduates returned to their \nfirst year faculty and 12.3 per cent returned to a different faculty.  \nFigure 22: Proportions of First Year Domestic Undergraduates who Returned for their Second Year of Study (2007 to 2015)   \nSource: Strategic Analysis and Data Warehousing, Applicant and Enrolment Management Report, Retention Rates Table.  \n71.2% 69.4% 71.3% 70.3% 71.1% \n71.2% 72.8% \n74.2% 75.3% \n12.8% 13.7% 13.3% 14.3% 13.8% 13.4% 11.7% 12.2% 12.3% \n84.0% 83.1% 84.6% 84.6% 84.9% 84.6% 84.5% \n86.4% \n87.6% \n0.0%\n10.0%\n20.0%\n30.0%\n40.0%\n50.0%\n60.0%\n70.0%\n80.0%\n90.0%\n100.0%\n2007/08 2008/09 2009/10 2010/11 2011/12 2012/13 2013/14 2014/15 2015/16\nR\net\nu\nrn\ned\nReport Year \nReturned to Same Faculty Returned to a Different Faculty Total Retention Rate\nAnnual Report on Undergraduate Enrolment 2015/16 29 \n5.3. STUDENT RETENTION, YEAR 1 TO YEAR 2, INTERNATIONAL UNDERGRADUATE \nUnlike with domestic undergraduates, total retention rate among first year international undergraduates \ndeclined moderately over the past two academic cycles reaching 84.1 per cent in 2015/16.   \nCompared with their domestic counterpart, a higher proportion of returning international students transfer \naway from their first year faculty. Among the 2014/15 first year international students, 68.2 per cent returned \nin 2015/16 to their first year faculty while 15.9 per cent returned to a different faculty.  \nFigure 23: Proportions of First Year International Undergraduates who Returned for their Second Year of Study (2007 to 2015)   \nSource: Strategic Analysis and Data Warehousing, Applicant and Enrolment Management Report, Retention Rates Table.  \n59.1% \n52.6% \n56.4% \n67.8% \n65.5% 64.3% \n68.7% 68.3% 68.2% \n22.8% \n19.9% 21.1% \n15.3% \n20.6% 21.0% 18.6% 16.9% 15.9% \n81.9% \n72.5% \n77.5% \n83.1% \n86.1% 85.3% 87.3% 85.2% 84.1% \n0.0%\n10.0%\n20.0%\n30.0%\n40.0%\n50.0%\n60.0%\n70.0%\n80.0%\n90.0%\n100.0%\n2007/08 2008/09 2009/10 2010/11 2011/12 2012/13 2013/14 2014/15 2015/16\nR\net\nu\nrn\ned\nReport Year \nReturned Same Faculty Returned Diff Faculty Total Retention Rate\nAnnual Report on Undergraduate Enrolment 2015/16 30 \n5.4. The total retention rate in 2015/16 is 77.9 per cent which is 2.3 per cent down from 2014/15. During the transition, a final report was noted as a \nnecessary summary of what effects were seen: Attachment 1 is that \nreport. \nThe Impact of the Proposal is See ‘Purpose’. \nReplaces/Revises (eg, policies, \nresolutions) \nN/A \nTimeline/Implementation Date N/A \nEstimated Cost N/A \nSources of Funding N/A \nNotes This report was discussed by the GFC Committee on the Learning \nEnvironment (CLE). Some committee members expressed concern \nabout a reduction in response rate after transitioning from the paper to \nelectronic format.  The committee noted that the data reported was \nlimited to a short time frame and that further data would be required to \nassess the long term impact to response rates. Another concern \ndiscussed involved the challenges in using the USRI (whether electronic \nor on paper) for multiple purposes. \nDuring discussion at the GFC Executive Committee, it was noted that \nsystem problems in large classes could occur if the eUSRI is being \ncompleted during class time.  The committee further discussed how the \nimportance of the USRI could be communicated to students, and \nwhether sufficient response time was given to students in light of the \nchanges in withdrawal dates. \nAlignment/Compliance \nAlignment with Guiding \nDocuments \nDare to Discover, Dare to Deliver  \nCompliance with Legislation, \nPolicy and/or Procedure \nRelevant to the Proposal \n(please quote legislation and \ninclude identifying section \nnumbers) \n1. Attachment 1 (page(s) 1 - 12): Evaluation of Online USRI Surveys 2014-2015 \nPrepared by: Scott Delinger, Director, Research Computing, Information Services & Technology \n 1 \nEvaluation of Online USRI Surveys 2014-2015 \nTest Scoring & Questionnaire Services (TSQS) \nSeptember 2015 \nSummary: A study of electronic USRIs (Winter 2015) and paper USRIs (Winter 2014) is reported below. \nConversion of USRI from paper forms to electronic means has resulted in median scores remaining the \nsame while average response rates dropped. Lab sections not previously surveyed were surveyed \nelectronically, but overall lab sections saw the greatest decrease in response. Lecture sections see milder \nresponse rate decreases. Comparisons of scores and response rates broken out for section type, faculty, \nand department follow in tabular format. The data in this report is a snapshot of one term for paper \n(Winter 2014) and one term for electronic (Winter 2015). \nIn Fall 2013, a pilot project was undertaken to offer Universal Student Ratings of Instruction (USRI) online \nfor face-to-face classes. The pilot project included 49% of the instructor-sections for the Fall 2013 term. \nSections in the pilot were approved to participate by the appropriate Dean or department Chair.  \n A summary of findings1 was presented in January 2014 including an analysis of response rate levels and \nmedia differences between online vs. paper-and-pencil operation. Research literature reports that scores \nobtained from online questionnaires are comparable to those from paper-and-pencil questionnaires while \nresponse rates are appreciably lower. The report concluded that the research literature findings also held \nat the University of Alberta. \nSubsequent to the GFC approval of modifications to GFC 111.3 (GFC 22 SEP 2014), IST implemented \nthe USRIs online administration campus-wide. Workshops and one-on-one training sessions were \nprovided to departmental contacts having responsibility for ordering the questionnaires. Some important \nchanges to note: \n- The window of administration for the online questionnaires is normally from the day after the \nwithdrawal deadline until the last day of classes (7 to 9 days). \n- A communication campaign focused on students was introduced using websites, Twitter, \nUniversity of Alberta Facebook channels, and screens in public places around campus. \n- USRI questionnaires were administered for courses with 4 to 9 students, using only open-ended \nquestions. \nDuring the academic year 2014/15 the USRI questionnaires were delivered online. The operation \nincluded: \n- Announcements to instructors (a week before the evaluation started) \n- Announcements to students (the day the USRI questionnaires open) \n- Reminders to instructors  \n- Reminders to students  \nThis report explores the response rate distribution and compares the results from paper-and-pencil with \nonline USRI delivery. Table 1 includes the number of instructor-sections for each term and delivery \nmethod. \nTable 1: Instructor-sections by Term and delivery method \nTerm USRI’s Delivery Instructor-sections \nFall 2013 Paper-and-Pencil 2114 \nOnline (Pilot Project) 2030 \nWinter 2014 Paper-and-Pencil 3860 \nFall 2014 Online 4618 \nWinter 2015 Online 4040 \n1 http://www.vpit.ualberta.ca/eusri/pdf/eUSRIFall2013Study.pdf \n 2 \nThe analysis compares the winter term of 2014 when USRIs were completed using paper-and-pencil to \nthe winter term of 2015 when the USRIs and the process were fully online. \nWinter 2014 \nIn January 2014, the directive from the Provost’s office was to conduct the USRI questionnaires using \npaper-and-pencil format. There were a total of 342 requisitions submitted for paper administration \nincluding 3860 instructor-sections. (Note there are instructor-sections for a single class where team-\nteaching is involved). \nCourses delivered over the Web with an online USRI questionnaire were excluded from the study. \nWinter 2015 \nAs established with changes to the GFC policy 111.3, the USRI questionnaires were conducted online. \nThe USRIs were available from the day after the withdrawal deadline until the last day of classes (April 2 \nto April 10 which included Easter break). Enrollment was updated daily during the administration window \nto exclude student withdrawals as soon as they were processed by the registrar office. \nFor Winter 2015, there were a total of 511 requisitions submitted for online administration, including 4040 \ninstructor-sections. The increase in the number of requisitions is due to additional lab-sections being \nevaluated and the administration of the USRI for courses with 4 to 9 students (open-ended questions \nonly) using the online system. \nCourses that required a paper-and-pencil USRI questionnaire (i.e. Field studies with no internet access) \nwere excluded from the study. \nIn the following tables, the main questionnaire items of interest that were explored were: \n1. Overall, the quality of the course content was excellent. \n2. Overall, this instructor was excellent. \nOverall comparisons of results from paper surveys versus online surveys are shown below in Tables 2-4. \nAll the following tables show the percentile distributions of response rates and median scores where \nthe 50th percentile indicates an average value and the 5th and 95th percentiles are given to show the \ntypical range of values. Values appreciably below the 5th percentile could be considered anomalies \nneeding individual investigation or explanation. \nTable 2: Overall Response Rates \nTerm \nN \nMin Max \nPercentiles \nValid Missing 5 25 50 75 95 \nWinter 2014 3860 0 3.3 100.0 35.5 64.3 78.0 90.0 100.0 \nWinter 2015 4040 0 6.3 100.0 30.8 50.6 62.1 76.2 95.0 \n 3 \nTable 3: Medians for Item: “Overall, this instructor was excellent” \nTerm \nN \nMin Max \nPercentiles \nValid Missing 5 25 50 75 95 \nWinter 2014 3860 0 1.8 5.0 3.4 4.1 4.6 4.8 5.0 \nWinter 2015 4040 0 1.0 5.0 3.2 4.1 4.5 4.8 4.9 \nTable 4: Medians for Item: “Overall, the quality of the course content was excellent” \nTerm \nN \nMin Max \nPercentiles \nValid Missing 5 25 50 75 95 \nWinter 2014 3247 0 1.5 5.0 3.3 4.0 4.3 4.6 4.9 \nWinter 2015 3240 0 1.0 5.0 3.1 3.9 4.2 4.6 4.9 \nWhile there was a 15% drop in response rates for online compared to paper surveys in this term \n(Table 2), the median scores for the items of interest were very similar (Tables 3,4). Organizationally, the office reports \nto the University Auditor to promote the independent, neutral, and \nadvisory nature of the function. \nIn 2010, the Helping Individuals at Risk Policy (HIAR) was introduced \nand an intake function was created to work in conjunction with the Office \nof Safe Disclosure and Human Rights (SDHR). The policy requires that \nannual reporting to General Faculties Council (GFC) and to the Board of \nGovernors will occur after each year of operation. \nThe purpose of this report is to provide an overview of the activities of \neach office and to comment on trends and issues in these.  \nThe Impact of the Proposal is To provide the University with an update on the activities of HIAR and \nSDHR and provide information on trends and strategies for dealing with \nthem. \nReplaces/Revises (eg, policies, \nresolutions) \nN/A \nTimeline/Implementation Date N/A \nEstimated Cost N/A \nSources of Funding N/A \nNotes N/A \nAlignment/Compliance \nAlignment with Guiding \nDocuments \nCompliance with Legislation, \nPolicy and/or Procedure \nRelevant to the Proposal \n(please quote legislation and \n1.
