The matter was \nreported anonymously and the concern was not within the \npurview of the university to investigate. \nETHICAL CONDUCT AND SAFE DISCLOSURE POLICY \nDISCLOSURES \nThe OSDHR provided intake and referral for 79 formal \ndisclosures during the reporting year.  \nCompared to past years, disclosures from \nacademic staff and students increased, while \nthere was a decrease of disclosures from \nnon- academic staff.  The most frequently \ndisclosed matters continue to be \ndiscrimination and harassment, but OSDHR \nreceived allegations on a wide variety of \nmatters including safety, fraud and the \napplication of collective agreements.  \nIndividual Making Disclosure 2014-15 2013-14 \nAcademic  23 11 \nNon-Academic (NASA) 20 34 \nStudent 30 25 \nFormer Employee 2 2 \nPostdoctoral Fellow - 4 \nOther (parent spouse, visitor, etc) 4 3 \nTOTAL 79 79 \nArea of complaint 2014-15 2013-14 2012-13 \nAcademic Appeal 1 2 1 \nAcademic Integrity - - 1 \nBreach of Collective Agreement 4 5 9 \nBreach of Contract - 1 1 \nBreach of Ethical Conduct & Safe \nDisclosure Policy \n4 5 3 \nConflict of Interest 1 - - \nDiscrimination 17 11 11 \nDuty to Accommodate and \nReasonable Accommodations \n6 6 4 \nHarassment 32 40 60 \n          Bullying 16 26 17 \n          General 11 5 26 \n          Gender - - 1 \n          Race 3 3 2 \n          Sexual 4 6 3 \n          Sexual Identity - - 1 \nPrivacy Concerns - - 1 \nSafety 2 2 3 \nTheft/Fraud 4 4 2 \nOther 8 3 3 \nTOTAL 79 79 85 \nOSDHR and HIAR Annual Report 2014-15 \nNovember 23, 2015   Page 3 of 12      \nREFERRALS \nMatters reported to the Office are typically referred to existing campus resources for resolution. The OSDHR maintains \na relationship with the various resource providers to ensure delivery of effective referral services. All matters brought \nforward have been referred to other University processes.  \nDue to the referral nature of the OSDHR, it is not possible to determine if all matters have been resolved to the \nsatisfaction of those involved.  Individuals are encouraged to contact OSDHR if they are not satisfied with progress. \nReferred To 2014-15 2013-14 2012-13 \nAcademic Staff Association   14 4 8 \nDepartment Chair/Faculty Dean/Vice-Dean 12 10 4 \nEmployee Relations  1 1 - \nFaculty Relations  2 - 1 \nFacilities and Operations 1 - - \nGraduate Students’ Association - - 1 \nImmediate Supervisor 3 - - \nInformation Purposes 1 5 8 \nInternal Audit Services 4 6 1 \nNon-Academic Staff Association  14 21 27 \nOffice of the Provost - - 1 \nOffice of the Student Ombuds 13 13 13 \nOrganizational Health & Effectiveness   2 2 5 \nOSDHR and/or Helping Individuals at Risk 2 3 1 \nProtective Services 1 1 3 \nResidence Services - 1 - \nSelf-guided resolution 1 5 2 \nSexual Assault Centre 2 1 1 \nStudent Accessibility Services  1 - 1 \nStudent Legal Services - 1 - \nStudents’ Union 1 - 1 \nNot Applicable 4 5 7 \nTOTAL 79 79 85 \nADVISORY SERVICES & CAMPUS COLLABORATION ON HUMAN RIGHTS \nThe OSDHR provides advisory services on matters of human rights and disclosure, both to individuals and as part of \nuniversity processes and governance structures.  The office provided advice to 156 individual students, staff and faculty \nduring the reporting year.  \nThe office also participated in university-wide activities such as: \n● Duty to Accommodate Working Group;  \n● Employment Equity Advisory Committee; \n● Duty to Accommodate Working Group; \n● Sustainability Awareness Week; \n● Equity, Diversity and Inclusion Week; \n● Supervisor Leadership Program; \n● New Employee Orientation & Info Marketplace; and \n● Week of Welcome (North and Augustana Campuses). \nOSDHR and HIAR Annual Report 2014-15 \nNovember 23, 2015   Page 4 of 12      \nTRAINING \nThe OSDHR Advisor delivered 31 training sessions to staff, students and faculty at North, Enterprise Square, Campus St. \nJean and Augustana Campuses.  Training was delivered at opportunities such as New Employee Orientation, Gold \nLeadership College, Augustana New Student Day, Residence Services Base Camp, the Supervisory Leadership Program \nand the Centre for Teaching and Learning’s Inclusive Learning Environments series (in partnership with the Student \nSuccess Centre). \nThe sessions were primarily focused on matters of human rights; however, disclosure services are also highlighted.  A \nseminar was hosted to promote the Public Interest Disclosure Act and inform participants of the university’s processes \nin relation to the Act. \nOSDHR translated some materials for use as Campus St Jean. \nCOLLABORATION \nThe OSDHR Advisor is called upon to collaborate with other post-secondary institutions as well as other organizations in \nAlberta and across Canada.  For the reporting year, these activities included: \n• Keynote address on “Human Rights in the Post-Secondary Context” at Medicine Hat College’s Human Rights \nDay activities; \n• Past President, Canadian Association for the Prevention of Discrimination and Harassment in Higher Education; \n• Chair and Emcee for the John Humphrey Centre Human Rights Awards; \n• Member of the Advisory Council of the Canadian Centre for Diversity and Inclusion, including involvement in \nthe develop of its professional designation; \n• Presentation on duty to accommodate at the Alberta Adapted Physical Activity Conference, Red Deer, Alberta; \n• Delivery of “Human Rights and Inclusion” workshop to a department within the Government of Alberta; and \n• Policy development in the area of accommodation and inclusion for the Fringe Theatre Festival Board of \nDirectors. \nWEB AND SOCIAL MEDIA \nOSDHR content within the disclosure domain (www.disclosure.ualberta.ca) had 4,767 unique page views.  The OSDHR \nhas an active Twitter account that is primarily used to promote human rights and disclosure matters that pertain to the \nUniversity. The account had 1,291 followers as of the end of the reporting year. \nREVIEW OF FUNCTION \nIn 2008, the University of Alberta’s Office of Human Rights was disbanded in favour of a new model to deliver \nhuman rights and safe disclosure services to the campus community. The Office of Safe Disclosure and Human Rights \n(OSDHR) was established and some services previously delivered by the Office of Human Rights were transferred to \nother units on campus, primarily Human Resources Services.  In 2015, WMC was contracted by the Vice-President \n(Finance & Administration) to evaluate the effectiveness of the new model, and to determine ways in which its \nperformance might be enhanced or changed.  A summary of the results is included in this report at Appendix B. \nOSDHR and HIAR Annual Report 2014-15 \nNovember 23, 2015   Page 5 of 12      \nUPCOMING PRIORITIES \nEDUCATION AND AWARENESS \nThe Office will continue to design and implement tools that assist in education and awareness activities. This includes \ndeveloping an online reporting and self-serve tools as well as continuing to embed disclosure and human rights \nprinciples into existing resources. The online reporting tool will be implemented in November 2015 to augment current \nOSDHR disclosure mechanisms.\nHUMAN RIGHTS VISITING LECTURESHIP \nIn partnership with Global Education, OSDHR will focus on reinvigorating the Visiting Lectureship in Human Rights. \nThis will include a review of the endowment’s terms of reference and an implementation plan for future events. \nASSESSMENT AND IMPLEMENTATION OF RECOMMENDATIONS FROM OSDHR REVIEW \nRecommendations contained in the OSDHR review will be evaluated. An implementation plan that outlines priorities, \ntimelines and required resources will be developed to address the recommendations.  \nOSDHR and HIAR Annual Report 2014-15 \nNovember 23, 2015   Page 6 of 12      \nGoals: \nto encourage the campus community to “Trust Your Instincts,” to recognize \nand report worrisome behaviours of those at risk of harm to self or others  \nto provide a confidential centralized location for reports of worrisome \nbehaviour and to connect situations that would otherwise seem to be \nisolated incidents, and assess the reported behaviours  \nto help connect Individuals at Risk to resources before a situation escalates \nREPORTS OF INDIVIDUALS AT RISK \nNUMBER OF INDIVIDUALS REPORTED \nThe HIAR Case Team Coordinator received 421 reports about 280 perceived Individuals at Risk, an increase from the \nprevious year in both the number of reports (16%) and the reported number of perceived Individuals at Risk (23%). \nThese numbers demonstrate a continued growth in the program. \nThe majority of reports about perceived Individuals at Risk were regarding undergraduate students (66%); 16% were \ngraduate students, 5% were faculty or staff, 4% were in open studies, 5% were about former students and the \nremainder (4%) were other (i.e. applicants, visiting or special students, or former employees). The majority of \nperceived Individuals at Risk were male (59%).   \nInternational Students comprised 28% of all perceived Individuals at Risk (staff and students), and 34% of students \nreported to the program; only a marginal increase from the previous reporting year (1%).  \nMost reports about perceived Individuals at Risk were about either a risk of harm to self (41%) or an exhibition of \nbehavioural concerns (41%) which did not meet the assessment of harm.  16% of the perceived Individuals at Risk \nwere reported to be at risk of harm to others, 1% were at risk of both harm to self and others and there was one \nreport about a disruption to the community. \n0\n100\n200\n300\n400\n500\nNumber of\nIndividuals at Risk\nNumber of Reports\n2010-11\n2011-12\n2012-13\n2013-14\n2014-15\nStatus of the Individual at Risk \nUndergraduate\nGraduate\nFaculty and Staff\nFormer Students\nOpen Studies\nOther\nOSDHR and HIAR Annual Report 2014-15 \nNovember 23, 2015   Page 7 of 12      \nThe number of reports about those exhibiting behavioural concerns - that is displaying those worrisome behaviours \nthat could not yet be defined as a clear risk of harm to self or others - was again significant this year. Reporters seem \nto recognize that although the behaviours are not yet a clear risk of harm to self or others, they would rather make a \nreport and have HIAR intervene earlier, or at least create a record of the behaviours, in case they escalate in the \nfuture. \nREPORTERS OF AT RISK BEHAVIOUR \nThe greatest number of reports came from University of Alberta Protective Services (128 reports or 30%), Residence \nServices (66 reports or 16%) and the Faculty of Arts (46 reports or 11%).  The Office of the Dean of \nStudents/University Student Services made 35 reports (8%) and the Faculty of Science made 26 reports (6%). \nThe great majority of reports came from staff members (319 reports or 76%), with 9% coming from senior \nadministration, 10% from instructors, 4% from students, and 3% from family members. \nThe busiest months \nfor receiving reports \nof At Risk Behaviour \nwere November \n2014, December \n2014, and April 2015.  \nThere continues to be \nno significant trends \nappearing for \nreporting peaks other \nthan the summer \nmonths having fewer \nreports. \nType of At Risk Behaviour \nHarm to Self\nBehavioural Concern\nHarm to Others\nHarm to Both\nDisruption to Community\nReporters of At Risk Behaviour \nProtective Services\nResidence Services\nFaculty of Arts\nOffice of the Dean of Students\nFaculty of Science\nOther Areas of Campus\n0\n10\n20\n30\n40\n50\n60\nJuly Aug Sept Oct Nov Dec Jan Feb Mar Apr May June\n2012-13\n2013-14\n2014-15\nOSDHR and HIAR Annual Report 2014-15 \nNovember 23, 2015   Page 8 of 12      \nSERVICES PROVIDED \nThe HIAR Coordinator often provides more than one service to a perceived Individual at Risk or to the reporter, \ndepending on the situation and the level of risk of the individual.  Outside of receiving a report for information \npurposes under the centralized reporting component of the HIAR policy (61%), the most common services provided \nwere to follow up with a situation (39%), provide referrals for the Individual at Risk (32%) and refer the case to the \nHelping Individuals at Risk Case Team (21%).   \nAll cases that involved concerns of harm to others were referred to the Helping Individuals at Risk Case Team for \nassessment. \nService Provided Number of Times % of Individuals at Risk \n2014-15 2013-14 2014-15 2013-14 \nInformation Purposes Only 172 142 61% 65% \nMonitor Situation 17 107 6%* 50% \nReferral for Individual at Risk 89 58 32% 27% \nReferred to HIAR Case Team 60 49 21% 23% \nFollow Up Required 110 37 39%* 17% \nContact Individual at Risk 42 34 15% 16% \nReporter/Victim Provided Referral 31 27 11% 13% \nCoaching/Advice for Reporter 45 22 16% 10% \nAssess Risk of Individual at Risk 14 16 5% 7% \nDiscuss Safety Measures 14 13 5% 6% \nRefer to or Attend Protocol Team 5 9 2% 4% \nProvide information on HIAR 2 8 1% 4% \n*The significant change in these numbers from the last reporting period to this one are due to a difference in \ndocumentation rather than a difference in service provision. The HIAR Case Team Coordinator created the more \nactive category of follow up to better capture the types of activities that were occurring (contacting the faculty or \nservice providers, gathering more information from the reporter, etc.). \nEDUCATION ACTIVITIES \nThe HIAR Case Team Coordinator made a concerted effort to create awareness of the program across campus \nincluding presentations to as many faculty and department council meetings as possible.  The education activities \nincluded: \n- Conducting 19 presentations about the program to the general campus community \n- Presenting to 11 Faculty and 15 Department Council meetings on North campus, Augustana and Campus St. \nJean \n- Participating in the Sexual Misconduct Review, Suicide Prevention Strategy Task Force and Violence \nPrevention Working Group \n- Translating HIAR program material to French for Campus St Jean. \nThe HIAR web pages were accessed by 744 unique visitors, a 10% increase over last year. The web page had 50 visits \non the day of the student death in HUB mall. \nOSDHR and HIAR Annual Report 2014-15 \nNovember 23, 2015   Page 9 of 12      \nPROCESS DEVELOPMENT \nHIAR continues to promote procedures to formalize threat assessments for emerging cases in collaboration with \nUniversity of Alberta Protective Services.  This included hosting a second Violence Risk Triage workshop and a \nComprehensive Violence Risk Assessment workshop for key stakeholders on campus.  \nHIAR was prepared for a formal review of the program as outlined in the HIAR policy which was to be led by the Office \nof the Dean of Students.  The focus of the Dean of Students has been to expand the program to consider academic \n“early alert” warning signs and therefore, the review of the current program was deferred. PwC had conducted a \nreview of components of the program in 2013 and recommendations to strengthen process documentation were \nimplemented. \nUPCOMING PRIORITIES \nINFORMATION SHARING PROTOCOLS \nThe HIAR Case Team Coordinator will establish a written protocol for information sharing between HIAR and Protocol \n91, HIAR and University of Alberta Protective Services and HIAR and Counselling and Clinical Services as recommended \nafter the review of the student deaths on campus last year. \nEDUCATION, AWARENESS AND INTAKE \nHIAR, coupled with the Office of Safe Disclosure and Human Rights, is continuing to implement a communication plan \nto enhance education and awareness of the HIAR program, including online videos regarding the HIAR program, and \nthe development of an online reporting tool.   \nIn response to the increasing number of reports to HIAR, a (0.5 FTE) Helping Individuals at Risk coordinator was put in \nplace for September 2015 to support the program’s intake. \nIntake and program awareness will remain the main focus of the HIAR staff for the upcoming year. \nOSDHR and HIAR Annual Report 2014-15 \nNovember 23, 2015   Page 10 of 12      \nAPPENDIX  – SUMMARY OF THE REVIEW OF SAFE DISCLOSURE AND HUMAN RIGHTS SERVICES  \nINTRODUCTION \nIn 2008, the University of Alberta’s Office of Human Rights was disbanded in favour of a new model to deliver \nhuman rights and safe disclosure services to the campus community. The Office of Safe Disclosure and Human \nRights (OSDHR) was established with a mandate to develop and implement a more neutral and decentralized \nmodel of service delivery. Some services previously delivered by the Office of Human Rights were transferred to \nother units on campus, primarily Human Resources Services. \nWith the introduction of the new office, services of the previous Office of Human Rights were distributed to: \n The OSDHR: mandated to offer intake and referral services for concerns regarding conduct that does not meet \nthe University’s ethical standards, including concerns relating to human rights.  It was also mandated to \npromote a culture to ensure human rights principles are integrated into daily campus life. \n Human Resource Services: assumed the roles of employment equity, mediation of staff disputes and learning \nand development related to human rights issues. \nThe intent was to create a distinction between intake and advice for disclosure and human rights issues, and the \ncase management of individual issues which may be more appropriately dealt with through existing dispute \nresolution and specialized investigative/review services. \nAs per part of the agreement made at the time of the implementation of the new model (to perform a review of its \neffectiveness after it had been operational for five years), WMC was contracted to evaluate the effectiveness of the \nnew model, and to determine ways in which its performance might be enhanced or changed. The following is a \nsummary of their final report.  This summary is verbatim from the report.  Sections that identified participants in the \nreport, repeated information, or consisted of background information were not included in this summary. \nMaterial for this analysis was gathered from a number of sources including an extensive interview program, a \nreview of relevant documentation, detailed comparator research and an assessment of performance of the \ndecentralized services previously part of the Office of Human Right’s mandate. \nHAS THE CHANGE BEEN SUCCESSFUL? \nWhen the new Office of Safe Disclosure and Human Rights was launched in 2009, and some functions moved to \nHuman Resource Services, the stated purpose for the change was documented and reported to the University \ncommunity. WMC’s assessment of performance against these criteria is examined in the report. \nStated Purpose for Change Assessment \n  Adapt to the expanded role of human rights, \nequity and safe disclosure needs on campus. \nSome changes or adaptations needed. \nProvide further efficiencies in service and \nFunctioning well in current structure. \nEnsure a more consolidated monitoring \nand reporting of complaints. \nSome changes or adaptations needed. \nOSDHR and HIAR Annual Report 2014-15 \nNovember 23, 2015   Page 11 of 12      \nStated Purpose for Change Assessment \nEnsure timely development of the \nUniversity’s Discrimination and Harassment \nPolicy and reasonable Accommodation \nFunctioning well in current structure. \nEnsure a greater integration of these services \ninto the HR and Student Services fabric. \nFunctioning well in current structure. \nAt the time of the change, a letter from concerned students was received that documented five concerns students had \nwith the change to the former Office of Human Rights. WMC’s assessment of the OSDHR’s performance against these \ncriteria is examined in the following table. \nStudent Concern Assessment \n  Lack of proper interim procedures Not applicable to this review. \nLoss of OHR functions Office has alleviated concern. \nLowered visibility and profile of human rights \nand equity on campus \nOffice has alleviated concern. \nLoss of important advocate for students Not part of Office’s mandate and should \nnot be pursued. \nLack of consultation and transparency Not applicable. \nCONCLUSION \nThe OSDHR and the University’s new model for service provision have been a successful initiative to improve \nawareness, build partnerships and provide a safer environment for students, staff and faculty at the University of \nAlberta. The office functions well with limited resources and a complex and interconnected stakeholder web. \nSome risks to ongoing sustainability and future growth have been identified, and it will be important to ensure that \nthe Office continues to evolve and expand to meet these challenges. To maximize the benefits and reach of the \nOffice, the following recommendations have been developed to assist in the continued provision of excellent \nservice, in meeting the requirements outlined in its mandate, and model the principles that drove its creation. \nRECOMMENDATIONS \nIt is recommended: \n1) That the current structure of the OSDHR, its triage model of service delivery and reporting \nrelationship to the Board of Governors, be maintained. \n2) That means be found to more publicly demonstrate the University’s Senior Administration’s support for the \nwork of the OSDHR. \n3) That an Advisory Committee be considered, where appropriate to provide guidance regarding systemic issues \nand opportunities within the University of Alberta, as well as review trends in the broader community. The \nAdvisory Committee could be established as a subcommittee or task force of a current Committee (such as \nthe Equity Committee). If established, this committee needs a closely defined mandate as well as a ‘sunset \nclause’ to be invoked when the committee has met its mandate. \n4) That more robust performance measures be developed that track the ‘value’ of outcomes, as well as the \nvolume of work.  This will be a challenge for the Office, given the competing challenge of maintaining \nOSDHR and HIAR Annual Report 2014-15 \nNovember 23, 2015   Page 12 of 12      \nconfidentiality while also tracking outcomes in a decentralized case management environment. \n5) That greater attention is paid to monitor the intake volume in the OSDHR of the three key stakeholder \ngroups of student, staff and faculty; and that efforts be made to adjust the focus of outreach services as \ndeemed necessary. \n6) That the budget cuts to the mediation budget [in Human Resource Services] be re-evaluated to \nensure that people are not deterred from accessing the service by cost considerations, and that it is \nwidely available to employees. \n7) The roles and responsibilities, and methods of support for learning and development outreach should \nbe clarified between OSDHR and Human Resources Services. \n8) That additional resources be dedicated to the OSDHR, to address long-term sustainability, support \neducation and communications programming and focus on systemic issues facing the campus community. \n9) That the Outreach and Education programs be expanded through the use of students and other \nvolunteers. \n10) That dedicated resources are allocated to focus on specific and distinct responsibilities: one to address \nsystemic issues across the campus community; and a separate resource tasked with intake, case \nmanagement and tracking of case resolution. \n11) That guidelines for formal working relationships with referral groups be established with set \nmethods of follow up and tracking among groups. \n12) That the OSDHR continue to monitor and review the policies and procedures to ensure they are relevant and \nmeeting the needs of the University community and report findings to the Office of the Vice-President \n(Finance and Administration). \nOVERALL RESPONSE \nOSDHR agrees with most of the recommendations put forward by WMC and will look for ways to enhance to areas \nnoted.  In 2015-16 the office will: \n- ensure senior administrative support for the program is demonstrable, though we highlight that support has \nbeen longstanding; \n- develop an approach to ensure an effective advisory committee is introduced for the Human Rights \npriorities.  It is intended that the approach will align with requirements and expectations being developed \nthrough the new institutional planning process; \n- explore integration of outcomes measures with other groups. Communications will be enhanced and \nguidelines for formal working relationships with referral groups will be established; and \n- introduce a student volunteer framework to address staffing concerns. \n  GENERAL FACULTIES COUNCIL \nOPEN SESSION  \nMeeting of January 25, 2016 \nITEM 4 - New Members of GFC \nMOTION I: TO APPOINT/RE-APPOINT  [This motion may be proposed only by statutory members of \nGFC – VPs, Deans, statutory students or elected faculty members]:  \nThe following non-academic staff member elected by the Non-Academic Staff Association (NASA) for a \nterm concurrent with her term of appointment on the Board of Governors (December 15, 2015 to \nDecember 14, 2018):  \nFaiza Billo Human Resource Services \nThe following non-academic staff representative nominated by the Non-Academic Staff Association \n(NASA) for a term beginning immediately and ending June 30, 2018:  \nWei Liu University of Alberta International \nMOTION II: TO RECEIVE [This motion may be proposed by any member of GFC]: \nThe following ex officio member, to serve on GFC for a term beginning January 1, 2016 and extending for \nthe duration of his appointment (January 1, 2016 to June 30, 2016): \nJohn Beamish Acting Dean, Faculty of Science \nR:\\GO05 General Faculties Council - Committees\\GEN\\15-16\\JA-25\\New Members\\Item-4-Listing-of-Names-Jan25.docx \nItem No. 12 \nGENERAL FACULTIES COUNCIL \nFor the Meeting of January 25, 2016 \nGeneral Faculty Council Standing Committee Report \nExecutive Committee  \n1. OF GOVERNORS \nREPORT TO GENERAL FACULTIES COUNCIL (GFC) \nFOR THE GFC MEETING OF JANUARY 25, 2016 \nI am pleased to report on the following highlights of the Board of Governors’ Open Session meeting held on \nDecember 11, 2015: \nREPORT OF THE PRESIDENT \nThe President provided an update on his activities since October 16, including points on his November \ninstallation, building community engagement, the institutional strategic planning process, advancing specific \npriorities (including a residence guarantee for first-year students, the Maskwa House of Learning, and the \nscholarship for Syrian students), good news (the University’s three Rhodes Scholarships, second place in the \n2015 Falling Walls Lab, and the 2015 Alberta Science and Technology Leadership (ASTech) Awards), and \nrecognition of recent university faculty, student and staff honors and awards. \nHe included verbal points of interest to the Board on: \n• progress to date on the Institutional Strategic Plan (ISP), including roundtables and stakeholder meetings, \nFaculty feedback, the November 23rd joint Alumni Council Executive-Board of Governors-General Faculties \nCouncil-Senate planning retreat, the creation of a cross-institutional ISP working committee, the \nestablishment of 10 working themes, and the timeline for future community feedback and governance \ndiscussions; \n• recent administrative updates including: Provost Steven Dew’s recent move into the President’s office; \nBrendan Hokowhitu’s resignation as Dean of Native Studies; and Dean of Science Jonathan Schaeffer’s six \nmonth administrative leave; \n• the University’s response to media coverage related to a collared polar bear speculated to be a bear from a \nUniversity of Alberta research study; \n• a memorandum of understanding between the University and the City of Edmonton regarding construction of \na new arena on South Campus; \n• Enterprise Square’s current occupancy and potential future tenants; and \n• the recently established President's Award for Refugees and Displaced Persons Fund. \nBOARD OF GOVERNORS’ APPROVED MOTIONS \nOn the recommendation of the Board Finance and Property Committee, the Board of Governors approved: \n• that tuition that falls within the tuition fee regulation for 2016-17 be set at the 2014-15 rates, as directed \nby the Government of Alberta; \n• the proposal from University Administration to increase international tuition fees by 1.6%, effective \nSeptember 1, 2016; and \n• the 2016-2017 Residence Rental Rate Proposal, effective May 1, 2016. \nFollowing a review of a motion approved by the Board Finance and Property Committee with delegated authority \nof the Board of Governors, the Board of Governors approved motions to:  \n• accept and Post-Secondary Learning Act (PSLA): The Post-Secondary \nLearning Act (PSLA) gives GFC responsibility, subject to the authority of \nthe Board of Governors, over academic affairs (Section 26(1). \n2. GFC Policy Manual: GFC requests that the GAC report annually to \nCouncil (Section 56.2 (General Appeals Committee) of the GFC Policy \nManual). The GAC is a committee established under Section 15 of the \nBoard/AASUA Agreement (Faculty) and, until 1977, was a GFC \ncommittee. Currently, it is one of several non-GFC committees \nrequested to provide an annual report to GFC. GFC requests that the \nreport include a statistical summary of cases and their dispositions and \nprotect the confidentiality of individual cases. \n3. GFC Terms of Reference (GFC Procedures (GFC Agendas) \n(Reports)):  “Reports not requiring action by GFC will be discussed by \nthe Executive Committee (with committee chairs in attendance) and \nplaced on the GFC agenda for information. If a GFC member has a \nquestion about a report, or feels that the report should be discussed by \nGFC, the GFC member should notify the Secretary to GFC, in writing, \ntwo business days or more before GFC meets so that the committee \nchair can be invited to attend. Rodgers Faculty of Physical Education and Recreation \nSix appeals were made under the provisions of Article 15 of the Faculty Agreement. This Article \nprovides for appeals of Faculty Evaluation Committee decisions to be heard by the General \nAppeals Committee (GAC), the membership of which shall be the Provost and Vice-President \n(Academic) or a designate as Chair; three members from the above Panel, none of whom shall \nbe from the same Faculty as the appellant; and two tenured staff members selected jointly by \nthe President of the University and the President of the AASUA, who shall be from the same \nFaculty as the appellant. \nThis year there were four tenure appeals, one was allowed by the GAC, one was granted a \nsecond probationary period, another one extended probation period by one year, and there was \none that was dismissed. There was one appeal of 0.75 increment, which was dismissed, and \none appeal of 0.5 increment was allowed and raised to 0.75.
